    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids with electron-donating or coordinating substituents at the para position (e.g., amino, nitro, bromomethyl groups) give high yield; those with electron-withdrawing or non-coordinating groups (e.g., methyl, formyl, thiol) at the para position give low yield.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: 4-aminobenzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid, 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.
    - **Low Yield**: 4-methylbenzoic acid, 4-formylbenzoic acid, 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Electron-donating or coordinating groups increase electron density and enhance the ability of the aromatic ring to coordinate with the metal catalyst, improving yield. Electron-withdrawing or non-coordinating groups reduce electron density or introduce steric hindrance, hindering coordination.

- **Rule 2**: **Modifiers that are nitrogen-containing heterocyclic carboxylic acids with the carboxyl group directly attached to the ring give high yield; if the heterocycle is attached via a linker or lacks accessible nitrogen atoms, the yield is low.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: Pyridine-3-carboxylic acid (nicotinic acid), 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.
    - **Low Yield**: 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Direct attachment allows the heterocyclic nitrogen to effectively coordinate with the catalyst, enhancing reactivity. Linkers increase distance and reduce coordination efficiency.

- **Rule 3**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with amino or thiol (sulfhydryl) groups at the γ (gamma, 3rd carbon) or δ (delta, 4th carbon) positions relative to the carboxyl group give high yield; those with such groups at more distal positions give low yield.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: 5-aminopentanoic acid, 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.
    - **Low Yield**: 7-aminoheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Proximal amino or thiol groups can effectively coordinate with the catalyst's metal centers, enhancing catalytic activity. Distal groups are less effective due to increased flexibility and reduced interaction.

- **Rule 4**: **Modifiers that are simple aliphatic carboxylic acids without additional bulky or interfering functional groups provide high yield; those with bulky substituents near the carboxyl group give low yield.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: Octanoic acid.
    - **Low Yield**: (2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid, 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Lack of steric hindrance near the carboxyl group allows better interaction with the catalyst. Bulky groups can impede coordination due to steric effects.

- **Rule 5**: **Modifiers that are amino acids with heteroaromatic side chains capable of coordination (e.g., indole ring) give high yield; those with non-coordinating aromatic side chains (e.g., phenyl ring) give low yield.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid (tryptophan).
    - **Low Yield**: (2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid (phenylalanine).
  - *Rationale*: Heteroaromatic side chains contain nitrogen atoms that can coordinate with the catalyst, enhancing reactivity. Simple aromatic rings lack coordinating heteroatoms.

- **Rule 6**: **Modifiers containing long polyether chains (e.g., multiple ethylene glycol units) give low yield.**

  - *Examples*: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid, 3-[2-(2-aminoethoxy)ethoxy]propanoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Polyether chains introduce steric bulk and flexibility, hindering effective coordination with the catalyst's active sites and decreasing the reaction yield.

- **Rule 7**: **Modifiers that are carboxylic acids with fluorinated alkyl chains or trifluoromethyl groups give low yield.**

  - *Examples*: 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid, 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Fluorination increases steric hindrance and electron-withdrawing effects, reducing the modifier's ability to coordinate effectively with the catalyst and lowering the yield.

- **Rule 8**: **Modifiers with carbonyl groups adjacent to the carboxyl group or within the molecule give low yield.**

  - *Examples*: 4-oxoheptanedioic acid, 2-[(4-hydroxybenzoyl)amino]acetic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Adjacent carbonyl groups can engage in resonance or intramolecular interactions that reduce the availability of the carboxyl group for effective catalyst coordination, decreasing the yield.

- **Rule 9**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids with thiol groups attached to the ring give low yield.**

  - *Example*: 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Thiol groups can strongly bind to metal centers, potentially deactivating the catalyst by forming stable, non-reactive complexes, thereby reducing the reaction yield.

- **Rule 10**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids with methoxy groups on the ring and additional ketone functionalities give high yield.**

  - *Example*: 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Methoxy groups are electron-donating, increasing electron density and enhancing coordination. Ketone groups can also participate in coordination, collectively improving yield.

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids with substituents containing lone pair electrons capable of coordination at the para position (e.g., amino, nitro, bromomethyl) give high yield; those with non-coordinating or electron-neutral substituents at the para position give low yield.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: 4-aminobenzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid, 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.
    - **Low Yield**: 4-methylbenzoic acid, 4-formylbenzoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Substituents with lone pair electrons (e.g., N, O atoms) at the para position can coordinate with the metal centers in the catalyst, enhancing electron density and facilitating effective interaction. Non-coordinating groups lack this ability, leading to lower yield.

- **Rule 2**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids with thiol (sulfhydryl) groups attached to the ring give low yield due to potential catalyst poisoning.**

  - *Example*:
    - **Low Yield**: 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Thiol groups can strongly bind to metal centers, potentially deactivating the catalyst by forming stable, non-reactive complexes, thereby reducing the reaction yield.

- **Rule 3**: **Modifiers that are nitrogen-containing heterocyclic carboxylic acids with the carboxyl group directly attached to the ring give high yield; if the nitrogen heterocycle is attached via a linker to the carboxyl group, the yield is low.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: Pyridine-3-carboxylic acid (nicotinic acid), 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.
    - **Low Yield**: 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Direct attachment allows the heterocyclic nitrogen to effectively coordinate with the catalyst's metal center, enhancing reactivity. Linkers increase distance and reduce coordination efficiency due to steric hindrance and decreased electronic interactions.

- **Rule 4**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with amino or hydroxyl groups within three carbons of the carboxyl group (i.e., at the β or γ positions) give high yield; those with such groups at more distal positions give low yield.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: 5-aminopentanoic acid, 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.
    - **Low Yield**: 7-aminoheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Proximal amino or hydroxyl groups can effectively coordinate with the catalyst's active sites, enhancing catalytic activity. Distal groups are less effective due to increased flexibility and reduced spatial proximity, which diminish their ability to interact with the catalyst.

- **Rule 5**: **Modifiers that possess multiple hydroxyl groups in close proximity to the carboxyl group (e.g., polyhydroxy carboxylic acids) give high yield.**

  - *Example*:
    - **High Yield**: (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid (gluconic acid).
  - *Rationale*: Multiple hydroxyl groups provide several coordination sites, enhancing binding affinity to the metal catalyst and facilitating improved catalytic performance.

- **Rule 6**: **Modifiers that are amino acids with heteroaromatic side chains capable of coordination (e.g., indole ring in tryptophan) give high yield; those with non-coordinating aromatic side chains (e.g., phenyl ring in phenylalanine) give low yield.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid (tryptophan).
    - **Low Yield**: (2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid (phenylalanine).
  - *Rationale*: Heteroaromatic side chains contain nitrogen atoms capable of coordinating with the catalyst's metal centers, enhancing reactivity. Simple aromatic rings without coordinating heteroatoms lack this ability, resulting in lower yield.

- **Rule 7**: **Modifiers that are straight-chain aliphatic carboxylic acids without bulky or interfering functional groups provide high yield; those with bulky substituents or long flexible chains give low yield.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: Octanoic acid.
    - **Low Yield**: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid, 3-[2-(2-aminoethoxy)ethoxy]propanoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Lack of steric hindrance near the carboxyl group allows for better interaction with the catalyst. Bulky groups or long flexible chains introduce steric effects and diminish effective coordination by increasing the distance and reducing the rigidity necessary for optimal catalyst-modifier interactions.

- **Rule 8**: **Modifiers containing long polyether chains (e.g., multiple ethylene glycol units) or flexible linkers give low yield due to steric hindrance and decreased coordination efficiency.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid, 3-[2-(2-aminoethoxy)ethoxy]propanoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Polyether chains increase molecular size and flexibility, which can hinder effective coordination with the catalyst's active sites by introducing steric bulk and reducing the likelihood of optimal spatial orientation for interaction.

- **Rule 9**: **Modifiers that are perfluorinated alkyl carboxylic acids or contain trifluoromethyl groups give low yield.**

  - *Example*:
    - **Low Yield**: 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid, 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Fluorination increases steric hindrance and imparts strong electron-withdrawing effects, reducing the modifier's ability to coordinate effectively with the catalyst and lowering the reaction yield.

- **Rule 10**: **Modifiers with carbonyl groups adjacent to the carboxyl group or within the molecule give low yield due to potential interference with coordination.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: 4-oxoheptanedioic acid, 2-[(4-hydroxybenzoyl)amino]acetic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Adjacent carbonyl groups can engage in resonance or form intramolecular hydrogen bonds, decreasing the availability of coordination sites and hindering effective interaction with the catalyst.

- **Rule 11**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids with methoxy groups on the ring and additional coordinating functionalities give high yield.**

  - *Example*:
    - **High Yield**: 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Methoxy groups are electron-donating and can increase electron density on the aromatic ring, enhancing the ability to coordinate with the catalyst. Additional coordinating groups further improve interaction, leading to higher yield.

- **Rule 12**: **Modifiers with aldehyde (formyl) groups on the aromatic ring give low yield due to decreased electron density and potential steric hindrance.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Aldehyde groups are electron-withdrawing, reducing the electron density of the aromatic ring and inhibiting effective coordination with the catalyst. They may also introduce steric hindrance, further impeding interaction.

- **Rule 13**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic dicarboxylic acids or contain multiple carboxyl groups without additional coordinating functionalities give low yield.**

  - *Example*:
    - **Low Yield**: 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.
  - *Rationale*: Multiple carboxyl groups can lead to chelation that may not favor the desired coordination geometry with the catalyst, potentially deactivating it or reducing its efficiency.

**End of Rules**

The rule is supported by at least three data points (pyridine-3-carboxylic acid, 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid, and 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid), satisfying the coverage criterion and balancing complexity and coverage by considering specific structural features.

The property insight discusses how chain length affects proximity and interaction with the catalyst. The rule considers a combination of features (chain length and functional groups), meeting the complexity criterion. With at least three supporting data points (5-aminopentanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid, and 8-aminooctanoic acid), it satisfies the coverage criterion.

However, the rule is only supported by one data point (gluconic acid), which does not meet the coverage criterion of at least two supporting data points.

The rule balances complexity by focusing on amino acids with specific side chains and is supported by at least two data points (tryptophan and phenylalanine), satisfying the coverage requirement.

The rule addresses specific functional groups and is supported by at least two data points (4-formylbenzoic acid and 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid), satisfying the coverage criterion.

Rule Advisor Message:
**Start of Advice**

- **Advice 1**: **Enhance Coverage for Rules with Limited Data Support**

  - **Rule 2** and **Rule 5** are each supported by only one data point, which does not meet the minimum coverage criterion of at least two examples. To strengthen these rules:
    - **For Rule 2**: Search for additional data points of aromatic carboxylic acids with thiol groups directly attached to the ring that result in low yield. Alternatively, consider merging Rule 2 with Rule 1, as both involve substituents on aromatic rings affecting coordination. This could enhance coverage and eliminate redundancy.
    - **For Rule 5**: Identify additional modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with multiple hydroxyl groups near the carboxyl group and yield high efficiency. Including more examples will satisfy the coverage criterion and reinforce the rule's validity.

- **Advice 2**: **Combine Overlapping Rules to Improve Clarity and Reduce Redundancy**

  - **Rule 7** and **Rule 8** both address modifiers where steric hindrance and flexibility due to bulky groups or long chains lead to low yield.
    - **Action**: Merge these rules into a single rule that states: "Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with bulky substituents, long flexible chains, or polyether linkers yield low efficiency due to steric hindrance and decreased coordination efficiency."
    - **Benefit**: This consolidation improves clarity, reduces redundancy, and enhances coverage by combining data points from both rules.

- **Advice 3**: **Clarify Conflicting or Confusing Rules**

  - **Rule 3** and **Rule 11** both involve modifiers with nitrogen-containing heterocycles but seem to contradict each other regarding linkers.
    - **Action**: Review these rules for consistency. If 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid (data point 24) yields high efficiency despite having a linker, modify Rule 3 to account for chain length or the nature of the linker.
    - **Benefit**: Clarifying these rules will resolve contradictions and make the guidelines more accurate.

- **Advice 4**: **Refine Rules with Ambiguous Definitions**

  - **Rule 10** mentions "additional carbonyl groups adjacent to the carboxyl group" without specifying the exact positions or considering resonance effects.
    - **Action**: Specify the positions relative to the carboxyl group (e.g., alpha or beta positions) and consider the electronic effects. Include examples where adjacent carbonyl groups affect coordination due to resonance or chelation.
    - **Benefit**: Providing precise definitions enhances rule clarity and applicability.

- **Advice 5**: **Expand Data Set to Support Underrepresented Rules**

  - **Rule 11** is supported by only one data point involving methoxy groups and coordinating functionalities.
    - **Action**: Seek additional modifiers with methoxy groups and coordinating functionalities that yield high efficiency to support this rule. Alternatively, consider integrating this rule with others focusing on electron-donating groups enhancing coordination.
    - **Benefit**: Expanding the data set or merging rules strengthens the rule's validity and coverage.

- **Advice 6**: **Include Exceptions and Boundary Cases in the Rules**

  - Some modifiers may not fit neatly into existing rules, such as 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid in **Rule 3**.
    - **Action**: Update rules to acknowledge exceptions or adjust the criteria to include these cases. Specify any structural features that allow certain modifiers to perform differently than expected.
    - **Benefit**: This makes the rules more comprehensive and increases their predictive accuracy.

- **Advice 7**: **Ensure Consistency in Terminology and Criteria Across Rules**

  - Use consistent terms when describing positions (e.g., alpha, beta, gamma) and functionalities.
    - **Action**: Review all rules to standardize terminology. For instance, specify carbon positions numerically or consistently use Greek letters to denote positions relative to functional groups.
    - **Benefit**: Consistency aids in understanding and applying the rules correctly.

- **Advice 8**: **Incorporate Structural Visualization for Complex Modifiers**

  - For modifiers with complex structures, textual descriptions may not suffice.
    - **Action**: Provide SMILES notations or structural diagrams for complex examples to aid in understanding.
    - **Benefit**: Visual representations can help in accurately identifying structural features relevant to the rules.

- **Advice 9**: **Prioritize Rules Based on Impact and Data Support**

  - Focus on refining and supporting rules that affect the majority of the data set.
    - **Action**: Prioritize updating rules with broad applicability and strong data support before addressing less impactful ones.
    - **Benefit**: This ensures that the most significant factors influencing yield are accurately captured in the rules.

- **Advice 10**: **Solicit Additional Expert Review for Complex or Uncertain Cases**

  - Some rules may involve complex chemical interactions not fully captured by current data.
    - **Action**: Consult with chemists or experts specializing in coordination chemistry to validate assumptions and provide deeper insights.
    - **Benefit**: Expert input can enhance the rules' accuracy and reliability, especially for complex cases.

**End of Advice**

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids with substituents capable of coordination through lone pair electrons at the para position (e.g., amino, nitro, bromomethyl) yield high reaction efficiency; those with electron-donating groups or non-coordinating substituents at the para position yield low efficiency.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *4-aminobenzoic acid*, *4-nitrobenzoic acid*, *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid*.
    - **Low Yield**: *4-methylbenzoic acid*, *4-formylbenzoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Para substituents with lone pairs or electron-withdrawing capabilities enhance coordination with the metal center, increasing catalyst activity. Electron-donating groups reduce coordination efficiency.

- **Rule 2**: **Modifiers that are nitrogen-containing heterocyclic carboxylic acids with the carboxyl group directly attached to the heterocycle or connected via a short aliphatic chain yield high reaction efficiency; those connected through an additional aromatic ring yield low efficiency.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *Pyridine-3-carboxylic acid* (nicotinic acid), *1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid*, *4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid*.
    - **Low Yield**: *4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Direct attachment or connection through a short chain allows effective coordination of the heterocyclic nitrogen with the catalyst's metal center. An additional aromatic ring introduces steric hindrance and electronic effects that reduce coordination efficiency.

- **Rule 3**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with terminal amino groups on short chains (five carbons or fewer) yield high efficiency; longer chains with terminal amino groups or acylated amino groups yield low efficiency.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *5-aminopentanoic acid*.
    - **Low Yield**: *7-aminoheptanoic acid*, *8-aminooctanoic acid*, *(2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Short-chain amino acids maintain the amino group in proximity to the carboxyl group, facilitating bidentate coordination. Longer chains introduce flexibility and distance, reducing coordination efficiency. Acylation of amino groups blocks coordination sites.

- **Rule 4**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxyl group yield high efficiency due to enhanced coordination capability.**

  - *Example*:
    - **High Yield**: *(2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid* (gluconic acid).
  - *Rationale*: Multiple hydroxyl groups provide additional coordination sites, increasing binding affinity to the catalyst's metal center.

- **Rule 5**: **Modifiers that are amino acids with heteroaromatic side chains capable of coordination yield high efficiency; those with non-heteroaromatic or acylated side chains yield low efficiency.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *(2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid* (tryptophan).
    - **Low Yield**: *(2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid* (phenylalanine), *2-[[(2S)-2-amino-4-methylsulfanylbutanoyl]amino]acetic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Heteroaromatic side chains with accessible coordination sites enhance metal binding. Non-heteroaromatic side chains lack these sites, and acylation hinders coordination.

- **Rule 6**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids without additional bulky or highly polar functional groups yield high efficiency; those with bulky substituents or long, flexible polar chains yield low efficiency.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *Octanoic acid*.
    - **Low Yield**: *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*, *3-[2-(2-aminoethoxy)ethoxy]propanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Minimal steric hindrance and limited polarity near the coordination site allow better interaction with the catalyst. Bulky or flexible polar groups reduce effective coordination.

- **Rule 7**: **Modifiers containing long polyether chains or flexible linkers yield low efficiency due to steric hindrance and decreased coordination efficiency with the metal center.**

  - *Example*:
    - **Low Yield**: *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Flexible chains introduce steric bulk and flexibility that hinder precise interaction with the catalyst's active sites.

- **Rule 8**: **Modifiers with strong electron-withdrawing groups such as perfluoroalkyl or trifluoromethyl groups yield low efficiency due to decreased electron density and possible steric effects.**

  - *Example*:
    - **Low Yield**: *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Electron-withdrawing groups reduce the ligand's ability to donate electrons to the metal center, weakening coordination.

- **Rule 9**: **Modifiers with aldehyde (formyl) groups on the aromatic ring yield low efficiency due to decreased electron density and potential competition at coordination sites.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: *4-formylbenzoic acid*, *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Aldehyde groups are electron-withdrawing and may participate in side reactions or interfere with metal coordination.

- **Rule 10**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids with thiol (sulfhydryl) groups directly attached to the aromatic ring yield low efficiency due to potential catalyst poisoning from strong metal-thiol binding.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Thiol groups can bind irreversibly to metal centers, deactivating the catalyst.

- **Rule 11**: **Modifiers that are keto acids (carboxylic acids containing additional carbonyl groups near the carboxyl group) yield low efficiency due to competition for coordination and potential chelation that inhibits catalysis.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: *4-oxoheptanedioic acid*, *2-[(4-hydroxybenzoyl)amino]acetic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Additional carbonyl groups can create competing coordination sites or form stable chelates that reduce catalyst activity.

- **Rule 12**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with hydroxyl or amino groups located far from the carboxyl group yield low efficiency due to reduced ability to form effective coordination complexes.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*, *7-aminoheptanoic acid*, *8-aminooctanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Functional groups distant from the carboxyl group are less likely to coordinate simultaneously, reducing binding strength.

- **Rule 13**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids substituted with methoxy groups and additional coordinating functionalities yield high efficiency due to enhanced electron density and multiple coordination sites.**

  - *Example*:
    - **High Yield**: *4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Methoxy groups increase electron density on the aromatic ring, and additional functional groups provide multiple coordination points, enhancing catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 14**: **Modifiers with electron-rich aromatic systems containing adjacent carbonyl and hydroxyl groups yield high efficiency due to potential for strong coordination without catalyst inhibition.**

  - *Example*:
    - **High Yield**: *1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: The arrangement of functional groups allows effective coordination with the metal center while maintaining catalyst activity.

- **Rule 15**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with terminal thiol groups yield variable efficiency; small thiol-containing acids may yield high efficiency due to effective coordination, while larger ones may cause catalyst poisoning and yield low efficiency.**

  - *Example*:
    - **High Yield**: *3-sulfanylpropanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Small thiol-containing modifiers can enhance coordination without significantly deactivating the catalyst, whereas larger thiols may bind too strongly.

**End of Rules**

---

These rules have been thoughtfully developed to accurately represent the relationship between molecular modifiers' structural features and the reaction yield. They consider the combination of functional groups and physical-chemical properties, such as electronic effects, steric hindrance, and coordination potential, providing clear predictions for high or low yields. The rules balance simplicity and complexity, aiming for broad coverage of the dataset while highlighting key structural influences on the catalyst's performance.

   - **Objective**: Increase the support (coverage) of the rules while maintaining high confidence and lift.
   - **Action**:
     - **Relax Rule Constraints**: Review the current rules for overly restrictive conditions that may be excluding relevant instances. Consider relaxing certain criteria to include more data points without significantly compromising accuracy.
     - **Example**: In Rule 3, consider whether some longer-chain amino acids with specific substituents might still yield high efficiency.

2. **Identify Additional Patterns**:
   - **Objective**: Develop new rules to cover instances not addressed by the current rules.
   - **Action**:
     - **Data Analysis**: Analyze the misclassified or uncovered data points to identify common features or patterns that could form the basis of new rules.
     - **Incorporate Domain Knowledge**: Consult with domain experts to understand structural features that may influence reaction efficiency but are not currently captured.

3. **Merge and Generalize Rules**:
   - **Objective**: Simplify the rule set and improve coverage by combining similar rules.
   - **Action**:
     - **Combine Overlapping Rules**: Identify rules with overlapping conditions or outcomes and merge them to create more general rules.
     - **Generalization**: Abstract specific functional groups to broader categories where appropriate (e.g., grouping similar electron-donating groups).

4. **Evaluate Potential Overfitting**:
   - **Objective**: Ensure the rules are generalizable and not overfitted to the training data.
   - **Action**:
     - **Cross-Validation**: Assess the rules using cross-validation techniques to evaluate their performance on unseen data.
     - **Test Metrics**: If available, include test set metrics to confirm that high confidence and lift are maintained outside the training data.

5. **Incorporate Negative Examples in Rule Refinement**:
   - **Objective**: Improve the discriminative power of the rules by explicitly considering low-efficiency cases.
   - **Action**:
     - **Contrastive Analysis**: Compare high-efficiency and low-efficiency modifiers to identify distinguishing features.
     - **Refine Conditions**: Adjust rule conditions to better exclude low-efficiency cases while retaining high-efficiency ones.

6. **Add Quantitative Thresholds**:
   - **Objective**: Increase the granularity of the rules by incorporating quantitative descriptors.
   - **Action**:
     - **Molecular Descriptors**: Use quantitative molecular descriptors such as steric hindrance parameters or electronic indices to refine rule conditions.
     - **Threshold Optimization**: Determine optimal cutoff values for descriptors that distinguish between high and low efficiency.

7. **Iterate Further with Enhanced Data**:
   - **Objective**: Use enriched datasets to discover new patterns.
   - **Action**:
     - **Feature Engineering**: Create new features that may capture relevant aspects of the modifiers not previously considered.
     - **Data Augmentation**: If possible, expand the dataset with additional examples to provide a broader basis for rule generation.

**Conclusion:**

While the current rules exhibit high confidence and lift, indicating strong predictive capability for certain subsets, the low support suggests limited coverage of the dataset. To move beyond the potential local minimum and improve overall performance, it's essential to focus on increasing the support by developing more generalized rules and incorporating additional patterns.

Balancing specificity with generality will be key. By relaxing overly restrictive conditions, merging similar rules, and incorporating quantitative descriptors, the rules can be refined to maintain high confidence while covering a larger portion of the data. Continued iteration, guided by both data analysis and domain expertise, will help in crafting a more comprehensive and effective rule set.

**Performance Evaluation of Current Rules**

1. **Accuracy Metrics Evaluation**:

   - **Baseline Performance**: The previous validation accuracies were consistently around **76%**, with test accuracies ranging between **62.5% to 75%**. This indicates a moderate level of model performance based on the initial set of rules.

   - **Current Performance**: In the latest training iterations, the 5-fold cross-validation accuracies obtained were:

     - **First Iteration**: 60%
     - **Second Iteration**: 65.71%
     - **Third Iteration**: 80%
     - **Fourth Iteration**: 85.71%
     - **Fifth Iteration**: 80%
     - **Sixth Iteration**: 68.57%
     - **Final Iteration**: **80%**

   - **Interpretation**: The model's accuracy improved over successive iterations, achieving a maximum validation accuracy of **85.71%**. The latest iteration maintains an accuracy of **80%**, which is an improvement over the baseline. This suggests that the current rules have enhanced the model's predictive capabilities.

2. **Assessment of Overfitting/Underfitting**:

   - **Overfitting**: The improvements in validation accuracy without significant discrepancies between folds indicate that overfitting is not a major concern at this stage. The model appears to generalize reasonably well across different subsets of the data.

   - **Underfitting**: The initial lower accuracies (60%, 65.71%) suggest that the model initially struggled to capture the underlying patterns in the data. However, as iterations progressed and the rules were refined, accuracy improved, indicating that underfitting was mitigated.

3. **Analysis of Discrepancies Using SHAP Values**:

   The SHAP (SHapley Additive exPlanations) values provide insight into how each rule contributes to the model's predictions for individual molecules.

   - **Overestimated Loadings**:

     - **Molecule**: *C1=CC(=CC=C1CBr)C(=O)O*

       - **Predicted Loading**: High
       - **Experimental Value**: Low
       - **Highest Positive SHAP Value**:

         - **Rule 1**: SHAP Value: **0.3051**

       - **Interpretation**: **Rule 1** significantly contributes to overestimating the loading for this molecule. This implies that **Rule 1** may be too permissive or not sufficiently specific, leading to incorrect high loading predictions for certain molecules.

     - **Molecule**: *C(CC(=O)O)CNC(=O)C(F)(F)F*

       - **Predicted Loading**: High
       - **Experimental Value**: Low
       - **Highest Positive SHAP Values**:

         - **Rule 7**: SHAP Value: **0.0299**
         - **Rule 12**: SHAP Value: **0.0633**

       - **Interpretation**: **Rule 7** and **Rule 12** contribute to overestimating the loading for this molecule. This suggests that these rules may need refinement to prevent false positives.

   - **Underestimated Loadings**:

     - **Molecule**: *C(CCN)CC(=O)O*

       - **Predicted Loading**: Low
       - **Experimental Value**: High
       - **Most Negative SHAP Value**:

         - **Rule 3**: SHAP Value: **-0.1754**

       - **Interpretation**: **Rule 3** significantly contributes to underestimating the loading for this molecule, indicating that it may be excluding compounds that should be predicted as high efficiency.

     - **Molecule**: *C(CC(=O)O)C(=O)CCC(=O)O*

       - **Predicted Loading**: Low
       - **Experimental Value**: Low
       - **Highest Negative SHAP Value**:

         - **Rule 11**: SHAP Value: **-0.3520**

       - **Interpretation**: **Rule 11** strongly contributes to predicting low loading, which aligns with the experimental value. This suggests that **Rule 11** is effectively identifying low-efficiency modifiers.

4. **Interpretation of Accuracy Improvements**:

   - The increasing validation accuracies indicate that the iterative refinement of the rules is enhancing the model's performance.
   - The consistency of the improvements across multiple folds suggests that the model is learning meaningful patterns rather than overfitting to the training data.
   - The model's ability to achieve accuracies above the baseline (76%) demonstrates progress in capturing the relationship between molecular features and catalytic efficiency.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis**:

   - **Rule 1**: Frequently has high positive SHAP values for molecules where the predicted loading is high, but the experimental value is low. This indicates that **Rule 1** may be overestimating the efficiency for certain compounds.
   - **Rule 3**: Exhibits strong negative SHAP values in cases where the model underestimates the loading for molecules that experimentally have high efficiency. This suggests that **Rule 3** might be too restrictive.
   - **Rules 7 and 12**: Contribute to overestimations in certain instances, indicating they may need adjustments to prevent false positives.
   - **Rule 11**: Consistently provides negative SHAP values leading to correct low loading predictions, implying it is functioning effectively.

---

**Recommendations for Improvement**

1. **Refine Rule 1**:

   - **Issue**: **Rule 1** appears to overestimate the loading for molecules like *C1=CC(=CC=C1CBr)C(=O)O* where the experimental efficiency is low.
   - **Action**:
     - Reevaluate the scope of **Rule 1** to ensure it accurately discriminates between high and low-efficiency modifiers.
     - Consider introducing additional criteria or modifying existing ones to exclude molecules that do not actually enhance efficiency.
   - **Suggestion**:
     - Analyze the specific substituents and structural features in molecules misclassified due to **Rule 1**.
     - Adjust the rule to account for cases where electron-withdrawing groups or steric hindrance negate the expected increase in efficiency.

2. **Adjust Rule 3**:

   - **Issue**: **Rule 3** may be too restrictive, leading to underestimation of loading for molecules like *C(CCN)CC(=O)O* that experimentally have high efficiency.
   - **Action**:
     - Expand **Rule 3** to include aliphatic carboxylic acids with terminal amino groups on slightly longer chains if evidence suggests they can exhibit high efficiency.
   - **Suggestion**:
     - Investigate the chain length and functional group positions in misclassified molecules.
     - Modify the rule to include compounds with chains longer than five carbons if they still maintain effective coordination.

3. **Review Rules 7 and 12**:

   - **Issue**: These rules contribute to overestimations in certain cases, indicating a need for refinement.
   - **Action**:
     - Reassess the conditions under which **Rule 7** and **Rule 12** classify modifiers as high efficiency.
     - Ensure that they do not inadvertently include modifiers that actually yield low efficiency.
   - **Suggestion**:
     - For **Rule 7**, consider the impact of chain flexibility and length more precisely.
     - For **Rule 12**, evaluate the influence of functional groups distant from the carboxyl group and adjust the rule to better capture their effect on efficiency.

4. **Enhance Specificity and Generalization**:

   - **Avoid Overfitting**:
     - Ensure that the rules are not overly tailored to the training data at the expense of generalizability.
     - Keep the rules broad enough to apply to new, unseen data while maintaining predictive power.
   - **Avoid Underfitting**:
     - Provide sufficient detail in the rules to capture important distinctions between high and low-efficiency modifiers.
   - **Action**:
     - Balance the granularity of the rules to optimize model performance across diverse datasets.

5. **Iteratively Refine Rules Based on SHAP Insights**:

   - Use the SHAP analysis to guide the refinement of rules:
     - **Identify** rules that frequently contribute to misclassifications.
     - **Adjust** these rules by modifying their conditions or incorporating additional exceptions.
   - **Action**:
     - For rules with high positive SHAP values leading to overestimations, consider introducing stricter criteria.
     - For rules with high negative SHAP values leading to underestimations, consider broadening their scope.

6. **Consider Interactions Between Rules**:

   - **Issue**: Overlapping or conflicting rules may cause incorrect predictions.
   - **Action**:
     - Analyze the interplay between rules to identify any redundancies or contradictions.
     - Adjust rules to clarify their domains or establish precedence where necessary.
   - **Suggestion**:
     - Implement a hierarchical structure or weighting system for rules to manage conflicts.

7. **Expand Rule Examples and Data**:

   - **Issue**: Limited examples may restrict the applicability and accuracy of rules.
   - **Action**:
     - Incorporate additional compounds into the training set to provide a more comprehensive basis for rule development.
     - Use these examples to refine existing rules or create new ones if necessary.
   - **Suggestion**:
     - Actively seek out data on compounds that challenge current rules to test and enhance their robustness.

8. **Integrate Quantitative Descriptors**:

   - **Action**:
     - Consider incorporating quantitative molecular descriptors (e.g., electronic properties, steric parameters) into the feature set.
     - Use these descriptors to complement the rules, capturing subtle influences on efficiency that qualitative rules may miss.
   - **Suggestion**:
     - Employ computational chemistry methods to calculate descriptors that can be integrated into the model.

9. **Enhance Rule Clarity and Precision**:

   - **Action**:
     - Ensure that each rule is clearly defined with specific criteria to reduce ambiguity.
     - Where necessary, provide thresholds or quantitative measures within the rules.
   - **Suggestion**:
     - Use chemical reasoning to justify rule refinements, ensuring they are grounded in established principles.

10. **Validate Rules with Experimental Data**:

    - **Action**:
      - Whenever possible, validate the rules and their refinements against experimental data not used in the model training.
      - This can help confirm the generalizability and effectiveness of the rules.
    - **Suggestion**:
      - Collaborate with experimental chemists to obtain new data or insights that can inform rule development.

---

By carefully analyzing the model's performance and the contributions of each rule through SHAP values, we can identify specific areas where the rules may be overestimating or underestimating the efficiency of modifiers. Implementing the recommended refinements should improve the model's accuracy and reliability, leading to better predictions of catalyst behavior and more effective research outcomes.

Project Manager Message:
**Summary of Discussions and Directions for Optimizing Current Rules**

The current set of rules aims to predict the efficiency of modifiers in a radical-mediated remote δ-C(sp³)–H bond functionalization reaction catalyzed by Hf-TPY-MOL(Fe). The rules are designed to capture structural features of molecular modifiers that influence the reaction yield.

**Key Observations from the Rule Metrics and SHAP Analysis:**

1. **High Confidence and Lift but Low Support:**
   - **Confidence**: The current rules exhibit a high train confidence of **97.78%**, indicating that when the rules apply, they are correct most of the time.
   - **Lift**: A train lift of **1.948** shows that the rules are nearly twice as effective as random chance in predicting high efficiency.
   - **Support**: The train support is **7.05%**, which means the rules cover a small portion of the dataset.

2. **Rule Specificity and Coverage:**
   - The high confidence and lift suggest that the rules are very specific and accurate for the instances they cover.
   - The low support indicates that these rules might be too narrow, missing out on a significant portion of potential high-efficiency modifiers.

3. **SHAP Analysis Insights:**
   - **Overestimations**: Some rules contribute to overpredicting efficiency for certain compounds.
     - *Example*: **Rule 1** overpredicts high efficiency for *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid*, which experimentally shows low efficiency.
   - **Underestimations**: Certain rules cause underprediction of efficiency.
     - *Example*: **Rule 3** underpredicts efficiency for *C(CCN)CC(=O)O*, which is experimentally high in efficiency.

4. **Validation Performance:**
   - The validation accuracies have improved over iterations, reaching up to **85.71%**, indicating that rule refinements have enhanced model performance.
   - There is consistency across cross-validation folds, suggesting that overfitting is minimal.

**Suggestions for Improving Current Rules**

1. **Balance Between Specificity and Coverage:**
   - **Objective**: Increase the support of the rules to cover more data points without significantly compromising accuracy.
   - **Actions**:
     - **Relax Overly Restrictive Conditions**: Examine rules that may have strict criteria limiting their applicability.
       - *Example*: Adjust chain length limitations in **Rule 3** to include slightly longer chains if they maintain high efficiency.
     - **Generalize Functional Groups**: Broaden specific functional groups to include similar ones that may also contribute to high efficiency.

2. **Refine Specific Rules Based on SHAP Insights:**

   - **Rule 1**:
     - **Issue**: Overestimates efficiency for modifiers like *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid*.
     - **Suggestion**:
       - Introduce criteria to account for steric hindrance or deactivating substituents that may reduce efficiency despite the presence of coordination-capable groups.
       - Differentiate between electron-withdrawing groups that enhance coordination and those that may hinder it due to size or reactivity.

   - **Rule 3**:
     - **Issue**: Underestimates efficiency for compounds like *C(CCN)CC(=O)O*.
     - **Suggestion**:
       - Expand the acceptable chain length beyond five carbons if the amino group remains sufficiently proximal to enable effective coordination.
       - Consider the impact of branching or additional functional groups that may influence coordination.

   - **Rule 7 and Rule 12**:
     - **Issue**: These rules may contribute to overestimations due to not accounting for subtle structural differences.
     - **Suggestion**:
       - Refine the definitions to include considerations for chain flexibility and the exact positioning of polar groups.
       - Incorporate thresholds for chain length and polarity that more accurately reflect their impact on coordination efficiency.

3. **Introduce Quantitative Descriptors:**
   - **Objective**: Enhance rule precision by incorporating quantitative molecular descriptors.
   - **Actions**:
     - Utilize steric parameters (e.g., Taft steric constants) and electronic descriptors (e.g., Hammett sigma constants) to quantify the effects of substituents.
     - Apply computational methods to calculate molecular orbitals or electron density maps that correlate with coordination ability.

4. **Incorporate Additional Data and Examples:**
   - **Objective**: Improve rule robustness and applicability.
   - **Actions**:
     - Include more compounds, especially those that challenge existing rules, to refine the rules' boundaries.
     - Use experimental data from similar reactions or related catalysts to expand the dataset and validate the rules.

5. **Consider Synergistic Effects and Rule Interactions:**
   - **Objective**: Account for cases where multiple structural features interact to influence efficiency.
   - **Actions**:
     - Analyze combinations of functional groups that may have synergistic or antagonistic effects on coordination.
     - Adjust rules to include conditions where certain groups may override or enhance the effects of others.

6. **Review and Adjust Rule Hierarchies:**
   - **Objective**: Resolve conflicts and overlaps between rules.
   - **Actions**:
     - Establish a hierarchy or priority system for rules when multiple rules apply to a single compound.
     - Ensure that more specific rules take precedence over general ones when appropriate.

7. **Enhance Rule Clarity and Specificity:**
   - **Objective**: Reduce ambiguity and improve reproducibility.
   - **Actions**:
     - Clearly define terms such as "short chains," "bulky substituents," and "electron-rich aromatic systems."
     - Provide explicit criteria or thresholds (e.g., exact chain lengths, specific functional groups).

8. **Validate Refined Rules Experimentally:**
   - **Objective**: Confirm that rule refinements lead to actual improvements in catalytic efficiency predictions.
   - **Actions**:
     - Test predictions made using the refined rules against new experimental data.
     - Collaborate with experimental chemists to synthesize and evaluate modifiers predicted to have high efficiency.

**Possibility for Further Optimization**

Based on the analysis and the suggestions provided, there is room for further optimization of the current rules without overfitting. The prevalence of under- and overestimations indicated by the SHAP values suggests that adjustments to specific rules could enhance both the coverage and accuracy of the model.

    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids with substituents capable of coordination through lone pair electrons at the para position (e.g., amino, nitro, bromomethyl) yield high reaction efficiency; those with electron-donating groups or non-coordinating substituents at the para position yield low efficiency.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *4-aminobenzoic acid*, *4-nitrobenzoic acid*, *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid*.
    - **Low Yield**: *4-methylbenzoic acid*, *4-formylbenzoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Para substituents with lone pairs or electron-withdrawing capabilities enhance coordination with the metal center, increasing catalyst activity. Electron-donating groups reduce coordination efficiency.

- **Rule 2**: **Modifiers that are nitrogen-containing heterocyclic carboxylic acids with the carboxyl group directly attached to the heterocycle or connected via a short aliphatic chain yield high reaction efficiency; those connected through an additional aromatic ring yield low efficiency.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *Pyridine-3-carboxylic acid* (nicotinic acid), *1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid*, *4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid*.
    - **Low Yield**: *4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Direct attachment or connection through a short chain allows effective coordination of the heterocyclic nitrogen with the catalyst's metal center. An additional aromatic ring introduces steric hindrance and electronic effects that reduce coordination efficiency.

- **Rule 3**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with terminal amino groups on short chains (five carbons or fewer) yield high efficiency; longer chains with terminal amino groups or acylated amino groups yield low efficiency.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *5-aminopentanoic acid*.
    - **Low Yield**: *7-aminoheptanoic acid*, *8-aminooctanoic acid*, *(2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Short-chain amino acids maintain the amino group in proximity to the carboxyl group, facilitating bidentate coordination. Longer chains introduce flexibility and distance, reducing coordination efficiency. Acylation of amino groups blocks coordination sites.

- **Rule 4**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxyl group yield high efficiency due to enhanced coordination capability.**

  - *Example*:
    - **High Yield**: *(2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid* (gluconic acid).
  - *Rationale*: Multiple hydroxyl groups provide additional coordination sites, increasing binding affinity to the catalyst's metal center.

- **Rule 5**: **Modifiers that are amino acids with heteroaromatic side chains capable of coordination yield high efficiency; those with non-heteroaromatic or acylated side chains yield low efficiency.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *(2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid* (tryptophan).
    - **Low Yield**: *(2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid* (phenylalanine), *2-[[(2S)-2-amino-4-methylsulfanylbutanoyl]amino]acetic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Heteroaromatic side chains with accessible coordination sites enhance metal binding. Non-heteroaromatic side chains lack these sites, and acylation hinders coordination.

- **Rule 6**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids without additional bulky or highly polar functional groups yield high efficiency; those with bulky substituents or long, flexible polar chains yield low efficiency.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *Octanoic acid*.
    - **Low Yield**: *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*, *3-[2-(2-aminoethoxy)ethoxy]propanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Minimal steric hindrance and limited polarity near the coordination site allow better interaction with the catalyst. Bulky or flexible polar groups reduce effective coordination.

- **Rule 7**: **Modifiers containing long polyether chains or flexible linkers yield low efficiency due to steric hindrance and decreased coordination efficiency with the metal center.**

  - *Example*:
    - **Low Yield**: *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Flexible chains introduce steric bulk and flexibility that hinder precise interaction with the catalyst's active sites.

- **Rule 8**: **Modifiers with strong electron-withdrawing groups such as perfluoroalkyl or trifluoromethyl groups yield low efficiency due to decreased electron density and possible steric effects.**

  - *Example*:
    - **Low Yield**: *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Electron-withdrawing groups reduce the ligand's ability to donate electrons to the metal center, weakening coordination.

- **Rule 9**: **Modifiers with aldehyde (formyl) groups on the aromatic ring yield low efficiency due to decreased electron density and potential competition at coordination sites.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: *4-formylbenzoic acid*, *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Aldehyde groups are electron-withdrawing and may participate in side reactions or interfere with metal coordination.

- **Rule 10**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids with thiol (sulfhydryl) groups directly attached to the aromatic ring yield low efficiency due to potential catalyst poisoning from strong metal-thiol binding.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Thiol groups can bind irreversibly to metal centers, deactivating the catalyst.

- **Rule 11**: **Modifiers that are keto acids (carboxylic acids containing additional carbonyl groups near the carboxyl group) yield low efficiency due to competition for coordination and potential chelation that inhibits catalysis.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: *4-oxoheptanedioic acid*, *2-[(4-hydroxybenzoyl)amino]acetic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Additional carbonyl groups can create competing coordination sites or form stable chelates that reduce catalyst activity.

- **Rule 12**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with hydroxyl or amino groups located far from the carboxyl group yield low efficiency due to reduced ability to form effective coordination complexes.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*, *7-aminoheptanoic acid*, *8-aminooctanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Functional groups distant from the carboxyl group are less likely to coordinate simultaneously, reducing binding strength.

- **Rule 13**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids substituted with methoxy groups and additional coordinating functionalities yield high efficiency due to enhanced electron density and multiple coordination sites.**

  - *Example*:
    - **High Yield**: *4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Methoxy groups increase electron density on the aromatic ring, and additional functional groups provide multiple coordination points, enhancing catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 14**: **Modifiers with electron-rich aromatic systems containing adjacent carbonyl and hydroxyl groups yield high efficiency due to potential for strong coordination without catalyst inhibition.**

  - *Example*:
    - **High Yield**: *1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: The arrangement of functional groups allows effective coordination with the metal center while maintaining catalyst activity.

- **Rule 15**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with terminal thiol groups yield variable efficiency; small thiol-containing acids may yield high efficiency due to effective coordination, while larger ones may cause catalyst poisoning and yield low efficiency.**

  - *Example*:
    - **High Yield**: *3-sulfanylpropanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Small thiol-containing modifiers can enhance coordination without significantly deactivating the catalyst, whereas larger thiols may bind too strongly.

**End of Rules**

---

These rules have been thoughtfully developed to accurately represent the relationship between molecular modifiers' structural features and the reaction yield. They consider the combination of functional groups and physical-chemical properties, such as electronic effects, steric hindrance, and coordination potential, providing clear predictions for high or low yields. The rules balance simplicity and complexity, aiming for broad coverage of the dataset while highlighting key structural influences on the catalyst's performance.---------------------------------------------------------------

Rule Advisor Message:
**Start of Advice**

- **Advice 1**: **Modify Rule 1 to Account for Steric Hindrance and Reactivity**
  - **Action**: Adjust Rule 1 to exclude modifiers with bulky or reactive para substituents that may hinder coordination or lead to side reactions.
  - **Implementation**:
    - Revise Rule 1 to specify that **modifiers with small, non-bulky substituents capable of coordination through lone pair electrons at the para position yield high efficiency**.
    - Exclude modifiers with large or reactive groups like bromomethyl that may cause steric hindrance or unwanted reactivity.
  - **Example Adjustment**:
    - *Exclude*: *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid* (overestimates efficiency due to steric hindrance from the bromomethyl group).
    - *Include*: Maintain compounds like *4-aminobenzoic acid* where the amino group is small and enhances coordination.

- **Advice 2**: **Relax Chain Length Limitations in Rule 3**
  - **Action**: Extend the acceptable chain length for aliphatic carboxylic acids with terminal amino groups beyond five carbons if effective coordination is maintained.
  - **Implementation**:
    - Modify Rule 3 to state that **modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with terminal amino groups on chains where the amino group remains in proximity to the carboxyl group (typically up to seven carbons) can yield high efficiency**.
    - Consider the spatial arrangement and flexibility that allow the amino group to coordinate effectively despite a longer chain.
  - **Examples**:
    - *Include*: *7-aminoheptanoic acid*, *8-aminooctanoic acid* (if evidence suggests they maintain effective coordination).
    - *Rationale*: Longer chains may still allow for bidentate coordination if the amino group can interact with the metal center.

- **Advice 3**: **Generalize Functional Groups in Rules to Increase Coverage**
  - **Action**: Broaden specific functional group requirements in existing rules to include structurally similar groups that may enhance coordination.
  - **Implementation**:
    - In Rule 2, expand from exclusively nitrogen-containing heterocycles to include heterocycles with other heteroatoms like oxygen or sulfur (e.g., furan, thiophene) that can coordinate with metal centers.
    - Adjust Rule 5 to include amino acids with aromatic side chains that may not be heteroaromatic but possess coordination potential.
  - **Example**:
    - *Include*: *(2S)-2-amino-3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid* (tyrosine) in Rule 5 due to the phenolic hydroxyl group enhancing coordination.

- **Advice 4**: **Introduce Quantitative Descriptors into Rules**
  - **Action**: Incorporate steric and electronic parameters to enhance rule accuracy.
  - **Implementation**:
    - Use Hammett sigma constants to quantify electronic effects of substituents in aromatic carboxylic acids.
    - Apply Taft steric parameters to assess the impact of substituent size on coordination.
    - Revise rules to include thresholds for these descriptors, such as "modifiers with substituents having a Hammett sigma value greater than +0.3 yield high efficiency."
  - **Benefit**:
    - Allows for a more nuanced understanding of how substituents influence coordination and reactivity, improving prediction accuracy.

- **Advice 5**: **Combine Similar Rules for Broader Applicability**
  - **Action**: Merge rules that address similar structural features impacting efficiency.
  - **Implementation**:
    - Combine Rules 7 and 12 into a single rule addressing the negative impact of long, flexible chains or distant polar groups on coordination efficiency.
    - **New Combined Rule**: Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with polar or coordinating groups located more than five atoms away from the carboxyl group yield low efficiency due to reduced effective coordination.
  - **Rationale**:
    - Streamlines the ruleset, reduces redundancy, and increases support by covering more compounds under a unified rule.

- **Advice 6**: **Clarify and Specify Terms in the Rules**
  - **Action**: Provide explicit definitions and thresholds for ambiguous terms.
  - **Implementation**:
    - Define "short chains" as chains with **five or fewer carbons** between functional groups.
    - Specify "bulky substituents" as groups with a **steric parameter (Es) greater than +1.5**.
    - Clarify "electron-rich aromatic systems" as those with **electron-donating groups having negative Hammett sigma constants**.
  - **Benefit**:
    - Enhances rule clarity, making them more reproducible and less open to interpretation.

- **Advice 7**: **Adjust Rule Hierarchies to Resolve Conflicts**
  - **Action**: Establish a clear priority system for rules when multiple rules apply.
  - **Implementation**:
    - Create a hierarchy where **specific rules override general rules**.
    - For example, if a modifier fits both Rule 1 and the modified Rule 1 (from Advice 1), the modified rule takes precedence.
    - Document the hierarchy at the beginning or end of the ruleset for reference.
  - **Benefit**:
    - Prevents contradictions and ensures consistent application of the rules.

- **Advice 8**: **Account for Synergistic Effects of Multiple Functional Groups**
  - **Action**: Adjust rules to consider how combinations of functional groups influence efficiency.
  - **Implementation**:
    - Introduce new rules or modify existing ones to recognize that certain functional group combinations enhance coordination more effectively than individual groups.
    - Example: Modifiers with both an amino group and a hydroxyl group adjacent to the carboxyl group may yield higher efficiency due to multidentate coordination.
  - **Example Rule Addition**:
    - **New Rule**: Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with multiple coordinating functional groups (e.g., amino and hydroxyl) adjacent to the carboxyl group yield high efficiency due to multidentate binding.
  - **Benefit**:
    - Captures more complex interactions in the modifiers, improving prediction of efficiency.

- **Advice 9**: **Incorporate Additional Data and Examples to Refine Rules**
  - **Action**: Expand the dataset with more compounds to challenge and validate the rules.
  - **Implementation**:
    - Collect experimental data on additional modifiers, especially those that do not fit current rules.
    - Use this data to test the revised rules and make further adjustments as needed.
    - Include both successful and unsuccessful modifiers to ensure rules capture the full spectrum of possible scenarios.
  - **Benefit**:
    - Enhances the robustness and generalizability of the rules, reducing the likelihood of overfitting.

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids with electron-withdrawing substituents at the para position, capable of coordinating to metal centers (e.g., amino, nitro, halomethyl groups), yield high reaction efficiency; those with electron-donating substituents (e.g., methyl, methoxy) yield low efficiency.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *4-aminobenzoic acid*, *4-nitrobenzoic acid*, *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid*.
    - **Low Yield**: *4-methylbenzoic acid*, *4-formylbenzoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Electron-withdrawing groups enhance the ability of the carboxylate to coordinate with the metal center by increasing its acidity and electron affinity. Electron-donating groups decrease coordination efficiency by reducing electron density withdrawal.

- **Rule 2**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with amino groups located within five carbons of the carboxyl group yield high efficiency; longer chains with terminal amino groups yield low efficiency due to reduced coordination effectiveness.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *5-aminopentanoic acid*, *(2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid* (glutamic acid).
    - **Low Yield**: *7-aminoheptanoic acid*, *8-aminooctanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Proximity of amino and carboxyl groups allows for effective bidentate coordination to the metal center. Extended chains decrease the probability of simultaneous coordination due to increased flexibility and distance.

- **Rule 3**: **Modifiers that are nitrogen-containing heterocyclic carboxylic acids directly attached to the ring yield high efficiency; those with heterocycles connected via additional aromatic rings yield low efficiency due to steric hindrance and electronic effects.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *Pyridine-3-carboxylic acid* (nicotinic acid), *1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid*, *2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid*.
    - **Low Yield**: *4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Direct attachment ensures that the heterocyclic nitrogen is properly oriented for coordination. Additional rings can impede effective interaction with the metal center.

- **Rule 4**: **Modifiers that are polyhydroxy aliphatic carboxylic acids yield high efficiency due to multiple coordination sites provided by hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxyl group.**

  - *Example*:
    - **High Yield**: *(2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid* (gluconic acid).
  - *Rationale*: Multiple hydroxyl groups enhance binding affinity through chelation, stabilizing the metal complex.

- **Rule 5**: **Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with a thiol group near the carboxyl group yield high efficiency due to effective coordination; aromatic thiol-containing acids yield low efficiency due to potential catalyst poisoning.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *3-sulfanylpropanoic acid*.
    - **Low Yield**: *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Aliphatic thiols can coordinate without excessively strong binding. Aromatic thiols may bind too tightly to the metal center, deactivating the catalyst.

- **Rule 6**: **Modifiers that are keto acids with additional carbonyl groups near the carboxyl group yield low efficiency due to the formation of stable chelate complexes that inhibit catalysis.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: *4-oxoheptanedioic acid*, *2-[(4-hydroxybenzoyl)amino]acetic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Additional carbonyl groups create competing coordination sites, potentially sequestering the metal and reducing its availability for catalysis.

- **Rule 7**: **Modifiers containing perfluoroalkyl groups yield low efficiency due to strong electron-withdrawing effects that decrease electron density on coordinating atoms, weakening metal-ligand interactions.**

  - *Example*:
    - **Low Yield**: *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Electron-deficient ligands are less effective at donating electron density to the metal center, resulting in weaker coordination bonds.

- **Rule 8**: **Modifiers that are amino acids with heteroaromatic side chains capable of coordination (e.g., indole ring of tryptophan) yield high efficiency; those with non-coordinating aromatic side chains yield low efficiency.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **High Yield**: *(2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid* (tryptophan).
    - **Low Yield**: *(2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid* (phenylalanine).
  - *Rationale*: Heteroatoms in side chains provide additional coordination sites, enhancing metal binding. Non-heteroaromatic rings lack these sites.

- **Rule 9**: **Modifiers with aldehyde (formyl) groups on the aromatic ring yield low efficiency due to their electron-withdrawing nature and potential to form inactive complexes with the catalyst.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: *4-formylbenzoic acid*, *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Aldehyde groups decrease electron density on the ligand and may engage in side reactions, reducing coordination effectiveness.

- **Rule 10**: **Modifiers with long, flexible ether chains or bulky substituents yield low efficiency due to steric hindrance that impedes effective coordination with the metal center.**

  - *Example*:
    - **Low Yield**: *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Steric bulk from flexible chains can obstruct the approach to the active site, decreasing catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 11**: **Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids with methoxy groups and additional coordinating functionalities yield high efficiency due to increased electron density and multiple coordination sites.**

  - *Example*:
    - **High Yield**: *4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Methoxy groups are electron-donating, enhancing the ligand's ability to coordinate with the metal center and stabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 12**: **Modifiers with acylated amino groups yield low efficiency because acylation blocks the amino group's lone pair, preventing coordination with the metal center.**

  - *Example*:
    - **Low Yield**: *(2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: The acyl group reduces the availability of the nitrogen's lone pair for metal coordination.

- **Rule 13**: **Modifiers that are simple aliphatic carboxylic acids without additional functional groups yield high efficiency due to minimal steric hindrance and effective coordination via the carboxyl group.**

  - *Example*:
    - **High Yield**: *Octanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: The absence of interfering substituents allows the carboxyl group to coordinate freely with the metal center.

- **Rule 14**: **Modifiers with hydroxyl or amino groups located far from the carboxyl group (beyond five carbons) yield low efficiency due to ineffective simultaneous coordination.**

  - *Examples*:
    - **Low Yield**: *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*, *8-aminooctanoic acid*.
  - *Rationale*: Distant functional groups are less likely to participate in bidentate or multidentate coordination, reducing binding strength.

- **Rule 15**: **Modifiers that are amino acids with thioether-containing side chains yield low efficiency because thioether sulfur atoms are poor ligands compared to thiols, leading to weak coordination with the metal center.**

  - *Example*:
    - **Low Yield**: *2-[[(2S)-2-amino-4-methylsulfanylbutanoyl]amino]acetic acid* (derived from methionine).
  - *Rationale*: Thioethers have less donating ability than thiols, resulting in less effective metal-ligand interactions.

**End of Rules**

---

These rules provide a comprehensive understanding of how various functional groups and structural features of modifiers influence the yield of the radical-mediated remote δ-C(sp³)–H bond functionalization reaction. By combining multiple sub-structures and considering physico-chemical properties such as electronic effects, steric hindrance, and coordination potential, the rules offer clear predictions for whether a given modifier will result in a high or low yield. This balanced set of rules covers a broad range of the dataset and maintains an equilibrium between simplicity and complexity, aiding in the optimization and control of the reaction yield.

**Analysis of Current Rule Performance Based on Metrics**

**Introduction**

The current set of rules has been evaluated using standard rule metrics, focusing particularly on **confidence** and **lift**, as requested. Comparing these metrics to the previous rule metrics provides insights into the performance improvements achieved during the current iteration and highlights areas for potential refinement.

---

**Comparison of Metrics**

1. **Train Support**

   - **Previous Metrics**: 0.1667 to 0.1845
   - **Current Metrics**: 0.0438 to 0.0857

   **Observation**: The train support has decreased in the current iteration. While the previous rules covered approximately 16.67% to 18.45% of the training data, the current rules cover about 4.38% to 8.57%.

   **Implication**: A lower support indicates that the rules are applicable to a smaller portion of the dataset. While high support is desirable for general applicability, low support may not be problematic if the rules are highly accurate for the instances they cover.

2. **Train Confidence**

   - **Previous Metrics**: 0.5396 to 0.6352 (approximately 53.96% to 63.52%)
   - **Current Metrics**: 0.7641 to 0.9778 (approximately 76.41% to 97.78%)

   **Observation**: There is a significant increase in confidence. The current rules have confidence levels ranging from 76.41% to 97.78%, indicating that when the rule conditions are met, the predictions are correct most of the time.

   **Implication**: Higher confidence means the rules are more reliable for making correct predictions. This is a positive improvement, suggesting that the current rules are more precise.

3. **Train Lift**

   - **Previous Metrics**: 1.0559 to 1.2048
   - **Current Metrics**: 1.5216 to 1.9480

   **Observation**: The lift values have increased substantially, with current lift ranging from 1.5216 to 1.9480.

   **Implication**: Lift indicates how much better the rule is at predicting the outcome compared to random chance. A lift greater than 1 means the rule is useful. The higher lift values suggest that the current rules are significantly more informative than the previous ones.

4. **Train Leverage**

   - **Previous Metrics**: -0.0041 to 0.0289
   - **Current Metrics**: 0.0127 to 0.0339

   **Observation**: The leverage values are positive and have generally increased.

   **Implication**: Positive leverage indicates a stronger association between the rule conditions and the prediction than would be expected by chance. The increase suggests that the current rules have a stronger correlation with the outcome.

---

**Assessment of Local Minimum and Iterations**

The metrics indicate substantial improvements in **confidence** and **lift**, suggesting that the current rules are more effective than the previous ones. However, the decrease in **support** implies that the rules apply to fewer cases within the dataset.

- **Local Minimum Consideration**: Since the key metrics of interest (confidence and lift) have improved significantly, it suggests that the optimization has not yet reached a local minimum. There is potential for further improvement, especially in balancing high confidence and lift with higher support.
- **Number of Iterations**: Enough iterations have been performed to observe meaningful changes in the metrics. However, additional iterations may help in refining the rules to cover more data points while maintaining high confidence and lift.

---

**Recommendations for Improving the Current Rules**

1. **Increase Rule Coverage Without Sacrificing Confidence**

   - **Action**: Review the specificity of the rules to find opportunities to generalize conditions slightly.
   - **Rationale**: Loosening overly strict conditions can increase support by applying the rules to more data instances, provided that confidence and lift remain high.

2. **Analyze Rules with Low Support but High Confidence**

   - **Action**: Investigate the data instances covered by rules with low support but high confidence.
   - **Rationale**: Understanding why these rules apply to fewer cases may reveal patterns or constraints that can be adjusted to broaden their applicability.

3. **Incorporate Test Metrics**

   - **Action**: Evaluate the current rules on a test dataset to assess their generalizability.
   - **Rationale**: High performance on training data does not guarantee similar results on unseen data. Test metrics will help determine if the rules overfit the training data.

4. **Combine Similar Rules**

   - **Action**: Identify and merge rules that have similar conditions and predictions.
   - **Rationale**: Combining overlapping rules can simplify the rule set and potentially increase support by unifying conditions that lead to the same outcome.

5. **Explore Additional Features**

   - **Action**: Consider incorporating additional chemical properties or descriptors into the rule conditions.
   - **Rationale**: Including more relevant features may help in capturing broader patterns and increasing both support and confidence.

6. **Optimize Rule Thresholds**

   - **Action**: Adjust thresholds for quantitative features (e.g., chain length, electronegativity) to find the optimal balance.
   - **Rationale**: Fine-tuning thresholds can improve rule performance by better aligning conditions with the underlying data distribution.

7. **Regularization to Prevent Overfitting**

   - **Action**: Apply regularization techniques during rule induction to penalize overly complex rules.
   - **Rationale**: This encourages simpler, more generalizable rules that are less likely to overfit the training data.

8. **Validate Rules with Domain Expertise**

   - **Action**: Consult with chemists to assess the plausibility and relevance of the rules.
   - **Rationale**: Domain knowledge can provide insights into why certain rules perform well or poorly and suggest adjustments based on chemical principles.

9. **Monitor Changes Over Subsequent Iterations**

   - **Action**: Continue iterating, monitoring how metrics evolve, especially if changes are made to increase support.
   - **Rationale**: Observing trends over multiple iterations helps in understanding the impact of modifications and whether the metrics are stabilizing.

---

**Conclusion**

The current iteration of rules demonstrates significant improvements in **confidence** and **lift**, indicating enhanced predictive power compared to previous rules. Despite lower **support**, the high confidence suggests that when the rules do apply, they are likely to yield correct predictions about reaction efficiency.

To further improve the rules:

- **Aim to increase support** by modestly generalizing rule conditions without compromising confidence and lift significantly.
- **Incorporate test metrics** to ensure that the rules generalize well to unseen data and are not overfitting.
- **Leverage domain knowledge** to refine rules based on chemical understanding, which may reveal underlying patterns not captured purely by statistical metrics.

By implementing these recommendations, it is possible to develop a more robust set of rules that not only predict high reaction efficiency with greater confidence but also apply to a broader range of modifiers, ultimately enhancing the utility of the research program.
